  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_17cc985a-1fe5-4933-b30a-843ddfc1c040'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5540192610766229653">In Child of All Nations, the reader is immediately swept up by a story that is profoundly feminist, devastatingly anticolonialist—and full of heartbreak, suspense, love, and fury. Pramoedya immerses the reader in a world that is astonishing in its vividness: the cultural whirlpool that was the Dutch East Indies of the 1890s. A story of awakening, it follows Minke, the main</span>
  <span id="freeText5540192610766229653" style="display:none">In Child of All Nations, the reader is immediately swept up by a story that is profoundly feminist, devastatingly anticolonialist—and full of heartbreak, suspense, love, and fury. Pramoedya immerses the reader in a world that is astonishing in its vividness: the cultural whirlpool that was the Dutch East Indies of the 1890s. A story of awakening, it follows Minke, the main character of This Earth of Mankind, as he struggles to overcome the injustice all around him. Pramoedya’s full literary genius is evident in the brilliant characters that populate this world: Minke’s fragile Mixed-Race wife; a young Chinese revolutionary; an embattled Javanese peasant and his impoverished family; the French painter Jean Marais, to name just a few.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5540192610766229653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1670141862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1670141862" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1670141862?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=southeast-asian-fiction">southeast-asian-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1670141862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9822946979816259863">The second in Toer's humanistic, postcolonial epic. As with This Earth of Mankind, I spent a lot of time thinking "good lord, it's been ages since anyone's written a novel like this." Something with this degree of scope, of complexity. There's less romance and less family in Child of All Nations, and a lot more struggle. Struggle against the colonizers, struggle against the Javanese feudalists, struggles against the comprador classes, struggle against generally shitty people, struggle against on</span>
  <span id="freeText9822946979816259863" style="display:none">The second in Toer's humanistic, postcolonial epic. As with This Earth of Mankind, I spent a lot of time thinking "good lord, it's been ages since anyone's written a novel like this." Something with this degree of scope, of complexity. There's less romance and less family in Child of All Nations, and a lot more struggle. Struggle against the colonizers, struggle against the Javanese feudalists, struggles against the comprador classes, struggle against generally shitty people, struggle against one's own personal prejudices. Language becomes the key. Dutch is the language of the colonial state, Javanese is the language of the feudal past, especially in its highly Sanskritized form, English is the language of international discourse, and Malay is the language of the coming revolution, something both local and yet delinked from archaic tradition. Can't wait for the next volume.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3110817482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3110817482" />
    <a title="Sarita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita"><img alt="Sarita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505669779p2/62680378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3110817482?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarita" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita">Sarita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=indonesia">indonesia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3110817482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8264162703607075786">	In the first part of the Buru Quartet, Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind), our protagonist, Minke, a Native Javanese was attending an exclusive Dutch high school (HBS) and then married a beautiful Indo-European, Annelies, who is a daughter of a wealthy Dutch and his concubine, Nyai Ontosoroh.<br/><br/>"In humility, I realized I am a child of all nations, of all ages, past and present. Place and time of birth, parents, all are coincidence: such things are not sacred."<br/><br/>In the second part, Anak Semua Bangs</span>
  <span id="freeText8264162703607075786" style="display:none">	In the first part of the Buru Quartet, Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind), our protagonist, Minke, a Native Javanese was attending an exclusive Dutch high school (HBS) and then married a beautiful Indo-European, Annelies, who is a daughter of a wealthy Dutch and his concubine, Nyai Ontosoroh.<br /><br />"In humility, I realized I am a child of all nations, of all ages, past and present. Place and time of birth, parents, all are coincidence: such things are not sacred."<br /><br />In the second part, Anak Semua Bangsa (Child of All Nations), Minke has graduated and is learning the world from many people of different backgrounds. The closest one is his mother-in-law. She is self-taught; she has never been to any school, except that of life itself. She proves herself time and time again to be capable of defending her principles and self respect. She shows Minke what he can never learn at school.<br /><br />"You're an educated native! While Native people are not educated, it is you who must ensure they become educated. You must, must, must speak to them in a language they understand."<br /><br />The editor of the newspaper he used to write while attending HBS challenged him to write in Malay, the language of the Natives. This leads him to learn about his own society, the peasants, the injustice, the gap between theories and the reality, and more importantly about himself.<br /><br />"Good, you have admitted that. Now, if a Native starts to talk to you in high Javanese will you advise him to switch to low Javanese? Ha, you can't answer. You are still not able to give up the comforts and pleasures that are yours as an inheritance from your ancestors -rulers over your own native fellow countrymen. Yea're a cheat! The ideals of Liberty, Equality, and Fraternity of the French revolution?<br /><br />I shriveled in shame. Yes, I had to admit it: I was still unable to give up the benefits of my heritage. When someone spoke to me in low Javanese, I felt my rights had been stolen away. On the other hand, if people spoke to me in high Javanese, I felt I was among those chosen few, placed on some higher plane, a god in a human's body, and these pleasures from my heritage caressed me."<br /><br />The story is also about people fighting back, resisting the worst of colonial oppression and greed. The world is changing in the early 20th century. China is awakening, Japan is considered equal to the West, and the Filipino people have created the first Asian republic (although briefly).<br /><br />"We fought back, Child, as well and as honorably as possible."</span>
  <a data-text-id="8264162703607075786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_333219105" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/333219105" />
    <a title="Missy J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j"><img alt="Missy J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481102743p2/7406091.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/333219105?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Missy J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Missy J" href="/user/show/7406091-missy-j">Missy J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=indonesia-related">indonesia-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=asia-related">asia-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=read-in-indonesian">read-in-indonesian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=southeast-asia">southeast-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=2012-books">2012-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7406091-missy-j?shelf=2020-books">2020-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer333219105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11941563078247660073">This is probably my favorite book of Pramoedya Ananta Toer's Buru Quartet. Last year, I re-read Bumi Manusia and knowing the outcome of the story, I wasn't as fascinated by it like I was when I read it for the first time. Fortunately, this wasn't the case with Anak Semua Bangsa. I actually enjoyed this book much more during this re-read, especially in Bahasa Indonesia. <br/><br/>Minke, the protagonist is actually a very complex character and this time I could clearly see that he was brainwashed with colon</span>
  <span id="freeText11941563078247660073" style="display:none">This is probably my favorite book of Pramoedya Ananta Toer's Buru Quartet. Last year, I re-read Bumi Manusia and knowing the outcome of the story, I wasn't as fascinated by it like I was when I read it for the first time. Fortunately, this wasn't the case with Anak Semua Bangsa. I actually enjoyed this book much more during this re-read, especially in Bahasa Indonesia. <br /><br />Minke, the protagonist is actually a very complex character and this time I could clearly see that he was brainwashed with colonial mentality. Minke feels special for having received a Western education, which is understandable because he is a "pribumi" (native Indonesian), who most often were denied this privilege. But several of Minke's friends challenge him to break the mold and form his own identity. Jean Marais wants Minke to write in Malay so that his writing can reach a wider circle of local people. Instead of gracefully considering Jean Marais' suggestion, Minke gets offended! How dare a Frenchman who can't even speak Dutch, suggest that Minke should stop writing in Dutch. It didn't even occur in Minke's head to write for his own people. This is why Kommer (an Indo, which back then was a term to describe Eurasian) criticized Minke for not knowing/understanding his own people. Of course, Minke gets offended again but this time he actually takes into consideration what Kommer said, accepts his criticism and makes an effort to get to know his own people - the Javanese (this leads to the story of Trunodongso). <br /><br />Minke went on a vacation with Nyai to her village, where they stayed at the home of Nyai's brother Sastro Kassier. To the horror of Nyai and Minke, a similar fate like Nyai's has befallen her brother's own daughter Surati. Nyai is furious at her brother for allowing history to repeat itself, especially when he knew how much it hurt her. In the flashback we come across a plague, which ironically I am reading about in these times of coronavirus. <br /><br /><i>"Dusun di sebelah sana, yang telah ditinggalkan oleh Surati, telah dibakar oleh Kompeni, tumpas bersama pepohonannya yang berpuluh tahun dipelihara oleh penduduknya selama ini. Tulangan sendiri tak pernah dibakar. Dokter didatangkan dari seluruh Jawa untuk menumpas. Pabrik gula besar tak boleh tumpas karena cacar. Modal harus tetap hidup dan berkembang. Orang boleh mati."</i><br /><br />We see how Minke cannot imagine a country that isn't ruled by Europe. When he hears that Japanese people were offered equal rights like European citizens in the Dutch East Indies (Indonesia), because Japan has made technological progress and become a country to fear, Minke is absolutely amazed. But he is also surprised when he meets a liberal Dutchman, who informs him about what is happening right next door in the Philippines: the Filipino educated class wants independence. Minke is shocked. How can an Asian country that isn't Japan, ask for such a thing! Minke begins to realize that European civilization isn't as perfect as he was taught in school. Spain and USA strike a deal and betray the Philippines. The European law disregarded his Islamic marriage to Annelies and have taken her away in the cruelest manner. The law is designed to protect the interests of the European colonizers and their capital. The native people stood not a single chance.<br /><br /><i>"Seribu advokat tak bisa kembalikan anakku padaku," sekarang bukan saja suaranya, juga bibirnya [Nyai] gemetar. "Tak ada satu advokat pun bersedia mengurus perkara Pribumi lawan Totok. Tak ada cara di sini."</i><br /><br />So when Minke wants to pursue more education, he is faced with the terrible truth that he will just be a screw in a huge machine. to help the Dutch colonial government maintain control. Yet there's so much that he needs to still learn and those things are not taught in school. Minke finds out that the majority of newspapers in the Indies are funded by the sugar companies, who will never report on the abuse suffered by Javanese sugar farmers. Minke also meets a young Chinese man Khouw Ah Soe who illegally entered the Indies to remind the Chinese people on the archipelago that China needs to catch up with the West in terms of technology and governance. The setting of the novel is around the turn of the 20th century. Pramoedya Ananta Toer really brought to life pre-colonial Indies and the diverse people that inhabited that space. <br /><br />Anak Semua Bangsa literally continues right after Bumi Manusia ends. There's no pause. I was a bit confused when it came to the Robert Suurhof story since I had completely forgotten about him and that he was also in love with Annelies. Pramoedya Ananta Toer narrated this entire Buru Quartet orally to his prison mates when he was serving time on Buru Island (eastern Indonesia). He only managed to put it in writing several years later. While I was reading this book, I couldn't help but feel that Pramoedya Ananta Toer would've been equally outraged by what is going on in the world today, and he would've definitely found the right words to describe what is happening now. <br /><br /><i>"Bangsa-bangsa yang menolak kekuasaan modal akan mati merana dan lumpuh tanpa daya. Masyarakat yang melarikan diri daripadanya akan menjadi masyarakat jaman batu. Semua harus menerimanya sebagai kenyataan, suka atau tidak."</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11941563078247660073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2712453271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2712453271" />
    <a title="Sarita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita"><img alt="Sarita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505669779p2/62680378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2712453271?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarita" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita">Sarita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789799731241" href="/book/show/1398044.Anak_Semua_Bangsa">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=indonesia">indonesia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2712453271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3222159311005638122">In the first part of the Buru Quartet, Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind), our protagonist, Minke, a Native Javanese was attending an exclusive Dutch high school (HBS) and then married a beautiful Indo-European, Annelies, who is a daughter of a wealthy Dutch and his concubine, Nyai Ontosoroh. <br/><br/>"In humility, I realized I am a child of all nations, of all ages, past and present. Place and time of birth, parents, all are coincidence: such things are not sacred."<br/><br/>In the second part, Anak Semua Bangs</span>
  <span id="freeText3222159311005638122" style="display:none">In the first part of the Buru Quartet, Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind), our protagonist, Minke, a Native Javanese was attending an exclusive Dutch high school (HBS) and then married a beautiful Indo-European, Annelies, who is a daughter of a wealthy Dutch and his concubine, Nyai Ontosoroh. <br /><br />"In humility, I realized I am a child of all nations, of all ages, past and present. Place and time of birth, parents, all are coincidence: such things are not sacred."<br /><br />In the second part, Anak Semua Bangsa (Child of All Nations), Minke has graduated and is learning the world from many people of different backgrounds. The closest one is his mother-in-law. She is self-taught; she has never been to any school, except that of life itself. She proves herself time and time again to be capable of defending her principles and self respect. She shows Minke what he can never learn at school.<br /><br />"You're an educated native! While Native people are not educated, it is you who must ensure they become educated. You must, must, must speak to them in a language they understand."<br /><br />The editor of the newspaper he used to write while attending HBS challenged him to write in Malay, the language of the Natives. This leads him to learn about his own society, the peasants, the injustice, the gap between theories and the reality, and more importantly about himself.<br /><br />"Good, you have admitted that. Now, if a Native starts to talk to you in high Javanese will you advise him to switch to low Javanese? Ha, you can't answer. You are still not able to give up the comforts and pleasures that are yours as an inheritance from your ancestors -rulers over your own native fellow countrymen. Yea're a cheat! The ideals of Liberty, Equality, and Fraternity of the French revolution?<br /><br />I shriveled in shame. Yes, I had to admit it: I was still unable to give up the benefits of my heritage. When someone spoke to me in low Javanese, I felt my rights had been stolen away. On the other hand, if people spoke to me in high Javanese, I felt I was among those chosen few, placed on some higher plane, a god in a human's body, and these pleasures from my heritage caressed me."<br /><br />The story is also about people fighting back, resisting the worst of colonial oppression and greed. The world is changing in the early 20th century. China is awakening, Japan is considered equal to the West, and the Filipino people have created the first Asian republic (although briefly).<br /><br />"We fought back, Child, as well and as honorably as possible."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3222159311005638122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96902239" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96902239" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3022167-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375273189p2/3022167.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96902239?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/3022167-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96902239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13511265078846438452">This is the second volume of the Buru Quartet, a story about Indonesia in roughly 1900, and coping with /withdrawing from Dutch rule - the Dutch certainly don't seem to have much to recommend them as colonial masters. The story is about a Javanese writer/journalist who was married to a half-breed who was then taken away from him by her Dutch father's family, and then died - Minke, the main character continues to live with his strong-willed mother in law, a Javanese woman who overcame the hardshi</span>
  <span id="freeText13511265078846438452" style="display:none">This is the second volume of the Buru Quartet, a story about Indonesia in roughly 1900, and coping with /withdrawing from Dutch rule - the Dutch certainly don't seem to have much to recommend them as colonial masters. The story is about a Javanese writer/journalist who was married to a half-breed who was then taken away from him by her Dutch father's family, and then died - Minke, the main character continues to live with his strong-willed mother in law, a Javanese woman who overcame the hardships of being forced into a concubinage at an early age, and who certainly is one of the strong women of literature. I read the first volume on a prior trip to Indonesia, where I read the second volume - may hold the rest for another trip.<br /><br />This story does get preachy from time to time, and the basic story is soap opera-ish, but it does give a good sense of life under colonialism and emerging strains of nationalism (particularly as they are able to find out that the Philippines had revolted against the Spanish). <br /><br />Toer wrote/composed this while in prison for political reasons about 40 years ago (1973) and first relayed the story to his fellow inmates - given that no one knew when they were going to get out, a long serial story with diversions would have been welcome. In this book more so than in the first volume I did get the sense of this as a story that continues on - while the reading does not suggest an oral history per se, the content, and the lack of need to be concise, does come through. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13511265078846438452" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4838784789756401704">this is the second book of the quartet and i really wish i didn't wait so long after reading the first before reading this one. i read the first book almost a year ago or more and i couldn't remember some events which were kind of important. they weren't essential to the plot of the second but they were mentioned and i was dissapointed i couldn't recall them. i think i would've liked it even more had i been able to remember much of the first book. other than that though i really enjoyed this boo</span>
  <span id="freeText4838784789756401704" style="display:none">this is the second book of the quartet and i really wish i didn't wait so long after reading the first before reading this one. i read the first book almost a year ago or more and i couldn't remember some events which were kind of important. they weren't essential to the plot of the second but they were mentioned and i was dissapointed i couldn't recall them. i think i would've liked it even more had i been able to remember much of the first book. other than that though i really enjoyed this book, equally as much as i remember enjoying the first. minke and all of the other characters return and carry on in their various colonial/anti-colonial roles and discussions. minke learns he needs to be more involved with his people rather than with the dutch colonialist aristocracy he mainly associates himself with and his main dilemma throughout is whether he should write in dutch or his native language. if he writes in dutch or even english the world can read it but the people cant and also wont trust him, but he considers the native language of his country to be crass and thinks the people are mostly illiterate so they wont read his writings anyway. he basically is being compelled by circumstances as well as all of his friends and acquaintances to step out of his aristocratic/educated role and give up his ingrained misgivings about class in javanese society, similar to what he had to deal with in his misgivings about the role of women in javanese society in the first book.</span>

<div id="review_12028518" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12028518" />
    <a title="Danielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/80233-danielle"><img alt="Danielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315776245p2/80233.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12028518?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danielle" href="/user/show/80233-danielle">Danielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">radicals, (post)-colonial lit. majors</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12028518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18368986923638606442">I really liked this book. The further I get into the tetralogy the more I feel like the narrator. Fascinating, tragic, inspiring, and a page turner. He's both a feminist and a misogynist, a colonialist and a revolutionary. He sounds like a coherent deconstructionist. To know that such a person existed...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Dhea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3889971-dhea"><img alt="Dhea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481731609p2/3889971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/199035390?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dhea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dhea" href="/user/show/3889971-dhea">Dhea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789799731241" href="/book/show/1398044.Anak_Semua_Bangsa">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer199035390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11371551477037966042">This is the second book in the quartet of novels known to some as the Buru Tetralogy. Minke, a Native, while in the process of recovering from the loss he experienced before tries to understand more about his identity and an identity as part of a nation.<br/><br/>Minke, though educated at the prestigious Dutch HBS secondary school, must accept the fact that he really doesn’t know his own nation. He gradually realizes that the Dutch occupation did not intend to civilize the Indies, but to brutalize it and </span>
  <span id="freeText11371551477037966042" style="display:none">This is the second book in the quartet of novels known to some as the Buru Tetralogy. Minke, a Native, while in the process of recovering from the loss he experienced before tries to understand more about his identity and an identity as part of a nation.<br /><br />Minke, though educated at the prestigious Dutch HBS secondary school, must accept the fact that he really doesn’t know his own nation. He gradually realizes that the Dutch occupation did not intend to civilize the Indies, but to brutalize it and rob it of sugar wealth and autonomy. He finally sees the facts on society (his society) that he never thought before. He sees that the power of those sugar companies with its money politic can really destruct people. Meanwhile, he also struggles a battle with himself about the sense of liberality and egalitarian which he has already learned from school and books about French revolution and finds out that he couldn’t apply them within himself. <br /><br />In this book, Minke finally gets a greater understanding of the possibilities of independence and stronger voices with which to shout. Though the story might sounds a bit preachy, it does give a good sense of emerging strains of nationalism. Toer also gave a very intriguing example from the Philippines who had revolted against the Spanish by the time. <br /><br />Another way to learn about history in a rather different perspective from what we’ve got in our history books.<br /><br />"Tanpa mempelajari bahasa sendiri pun orang takkan mengenal bangsanya sendiri.” --Kommer<br /><br />"Kau pribumi terpelajar! Kalau mereka itu, pribumi itu, tidak terpelajar, kau harus bikin mereka jadi terpelajar. Kau harus, harus, harus bicara pada mereka, dengan bahasa yang mereka tahu.” --Jean Marais</span>
  <a data-text-id="11371551477037966042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christian Satrianta Putra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christian Satrianta Putra" href="/user/show/15921088-christian-satrianta-putra">Christian Satrianta Putra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15921088-christian-satrianta-putra?shelf=collection">collection</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1414667176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15757767670294026355">The adventure of Minke continues (along with the building of Hindia Nation) in this book, the second part of Buru Tetralogy. Pramoedya beautifully capture what might have happened in the beginning of 20th-​Century Hindia from a perspective of both aristocracies and proletariats. The rise of Japan and the revolution in Philippine impacted the stagnancy of Asia’s position in world league, kind of tickled colonialism a bit, a threat.<br/><br/><br/><br/>Living a post-Annelies life, Minke faced a dilemma of claiming him</span>
  <span id="freeText15757767670294026355" style="display:none">The adventure of Minke continues (along with the building of Hindia Nation) in this book, the second part of Buru Tetralogy. Pramoedya beautifully capture what might have happened in the beginning of 20th-​Century Hindia from a perspective of both aristocracies and proletariats. The rise of Japan and the revolution in Philippine impacted the stagnancy of Asia’s position in world league, kind of tickled colonialism a bit, a threat.<br /><br /><br /><br />Living a post-Annelies life, Minke faced a dilemma of claiming himself as a nationalist without even having an extensive knowledge of his own Hindia’s social condition and culture. This situation made him realizes to do more engagement with other locals and start writing in mother’s­-tounge language. It is so easy to relate the confusing nationalism with the question you might have asked to yourself: what is so Indonesia about Indonesia? What is so Indonesia about you?<br /><br /><br /><br />A very beautiful writing, Less romance, more ideology (rising socialism?). We could dig more the character intimacy of Nyai Ontosoroh, Marais, Kommer, Darsam, Panji Darman, Nijman, and Sastro Kassier. Si Gendut. This sequel is even better than ‘Bumi Manusia’!<br /><br /><br /><br />Thank you, Minke. <br /><br /><br /><br />CP</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11175522315223280519">The second volume is more meditational and reflexive...There is no much action (compared to the first volume) but there is more internal struggle, self-reflection and questioning. The now fully established journalist Minke has to solve various dilemmas: should he write only in the language of the colonizer (Dutch) or also in Malay and Javanese (for his people)? Should he side with the oppressors and their interests or should he side with the oppressed (peasants, women etc)? Should he accept unco</span>
  <span id="freeText11175522315223280519" style="display:none">The second volume is more meditational and reflexive...There is no much action (compared to the first volume) but there is more internal struggle, self-reflection and questioning. The now fully established journalist Minke has to solve various dilemmas: should he write only in the language of the colonizer (Dutch) or also in Malay and Javanese (for his people)? Should he side with the oppressors and their interests or should he side with the oppressed (peasants, women etc)? Should he accept unconditionally the Western civilization or should he reject it?<br />I liked a phrase he used in this volume: some people do not want to get involved in public affairs. It is as if they want to fall asleep and when they wake up back..the change already has happened without them. <br />Is by far one of the most satisfying (and deep) readings I have had in a long while. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6746412936232881676">I wish I could say this book was comprehensible. I had to read Child of All Nations for a college history class, and I'd be lying if I said I was sad that I completed it. The writing and overall plot was so difficult to follow, and I'd even go and say that there wasn't really a plot at all. A few chapters were interesting (but most weren't), and the ending was satisfying, but I feel it would have been more satisfying if the plot wasn't so convoluted. I give this two stars because the theme of th</span>
  <span id="freeText6746412936232881676" style="display:none">I wish I could say this book was comprehensible. I had to read Child of All Nations for a college history class, and I'd be lying if I said I was sad that I completed it. The writing and overall plot was so difficult to follow, and I'd even go and say that there wasn't really a plot at all. A few chapters were interesting (but most weren't), and the ending was satisfying, but I feel it would have been more satisfying if the plot wasn't so convoluted. I give this two stars because the theme of the book, colonization of Europeans in Java and the Indies, was impactful and eye-opening, and I appreciate I learned that much.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7069005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10892714393468566898">a very good introduction into Indonesia's history and to be aware of what kind of harm colonialism did to the society </span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3361028276" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3361028276" />
    <a title="Sophia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22814910-sophia"><img alt="Sophia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483867761p2/22814910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3361028276?book_show_action=true">Jun 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sophia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sophia" href="/user/show/22814910-sophia">Sophia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22814910-sophia?shelf=indonesian-books">indonesian-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22814910-sophia?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22814910-sophia?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3361028276&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3361028276" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17005736748816184961">The beginning of the book absolutely shocked me. Annelies Mellema died?? Was she really so weak and heartbroken? However, that was the only memorable part of the beginning. The rest of the beginning was slow to read.<br/><br/>It begins with Minke being encouraged by his two friends to write in Malay, so that his writing would have more influence on more people. Minke disagrees and instead becomes mad towards Jean Marais, his disabled painter French friend, who first suggested it. He becomes less offended,</span>
  <span id="freeText17005736748816184961" style="display:none">The beginning of the book absolutely shocked me. Annelies Mellema died?? Was she really so weak and heartbroken? However, that was the only memorable part of the beginning. The rest of the beginning was slow to read.<br /><br />It begins with Minke being encouraged by his two friends to write in Malay, so that his writing would have more influence on more people. Minke disagrees and instead becomes mad towards Jean Marais, his disabled painter French friend, who first suggested it. He becomes less offended, however, when Kommer later suggests the same thing. In the beginning, we are also introduced to Khouw Ah Soe, who is a Chinese illegal immigrant that came to the Indies to start a movement amongst the Chinese. It is a bit abstract exactly how he intends to do this. However, he has radical points of view on colonialism, power, and revolution. He believes that China needs to "rise up" and leave the ancient generations' ways, so they do not become defeated by the Japanese.<br /><br />Minke was instructed to write down the interview between Khouw and Njeeman, the editor of the newspaper. Later he realizes that the version of the interview which was published was completely different than what he witnessed. This is when his glorification of European knowledge becomes further disarrayed. <br /><br />The highlight of this book is when he visits peasants in the village during a vacation with Mama. During this vacation, he finds out also that Nyai's nephew was sold by her brother, just as she was once sold by her own father. The tragic story is recounted, and we come to understand more about how cruel relations between the Dutch and Natives were at that time. In this case, the plantation manager, named Plikemboh (meaning ugly penis) created a set-up wherein he accused Sastro Kassier, Nyai's brother who is the paymaster, of stealing the money that must be paid to the factory workers and plantation farmers. He did that so that Sastro Kassier would give up his daughter to Plikemboh, because Sastro Kassier knew that bringing Plikemboh to court would be in vain, since he was Native whereas Plikemboh was a Pure-blood European.<br /><br />During this vacation, Minke also visits one farmer, named Trudonongso, and his family, who live a simple life. Yet Trudonongso is angry because for years he has been cheated on by the colonizers who force him to give up his land. They cut off the water channels to his wet paddies. When he had no choice but to give up the land, they gave him less money than they had originally promised, but because he was illiterate and innumerate he could not prove this. Minke wants to give him justice by writing about him, and in consequence, all the other farmers who are like him. But when he shows the article to Njeeman, Njeeman disapproves of the article, claiming Minke has no proof of the misdoings he writes about. Later we find out the hideous truth: Njeeman is actually a shareholder (or beneficiary) of the sugar companies that mistreated the farmers. The newspaper Minke has been writing to all this time is also the child of the sugar company.<br /><br />We also find out that Herman Mellema did horrible things towards farmers as well in the past. He even ordered one farmer who would not comply to be murdered. Nyai has a breakdown because of this.<br /><br />What else? Oh yes, initially Minke wanted to go to Betawi, or Batavia, to study to become a doctor. During his ship he met with a radical/liberal Dutchman who used to be an editor for the Soerabaiiach Niews (not sure if I spelled that correctly), the newspaper Minke used to write for. This Dutchman rattles to him nonstop about nationalism, revolutions, capital, economics, and so on. Minke becomes enlightened. However, he doesn't make it to Batavia, because halfway there he is detained by the police. Why?<br /><br />Because apparently Robert, Annelies's brother, had an affair with Minem, one of the dairy workers of the Mellema family's business. Minem got pregnant and delivered a son named Rono, who is then given to Mama because Minem decided to live with an accountant. Robert dies in the US from the venereal disease he contracted from the Japanese prostitute. Before that, he traveled around the world as a sailor. He begs forgiveness from Mama, and confesses to agreeing to a plot set up by Babah Ah Tjong (manager of the neighborhood brothel) to kill all of the members of the Mellema family so that Robert could inherit the business. Babah would be the one taking care of it, while Robert would get the profits and live a leisurely life with his Japanese concubine, Maiko. Due to Robert's final letter confessing all of these things, the family gets called to court yet again. Thus Minke was unable to begin medical school.<br /><br />In the end, Maurits Mellema, Herman Mellema's son, visits them with the intention of demanding the inheritance. He finds out about Rono, his half-nephew. Everyone (Kommer, Jean Marais, Maysoroh, Minke, Mama, Darsam, and all the other villagers) becomes furious with him, accusing him of murdering Annelies and stealing the business and so they all burn him (not literally) nonstop. He becomes guilt-ridden and fearful, so he leaves. He postpones the date when they would have to leave the house and business.<br /><br />THE END!<br /><br /><br />Some fancy quotes:<br /><br />"The old people teach us through their legends that there is a mighty god called Batara Kala. They say it is he who makes all things move further and further from their starting point, inexorably, towards some unknown final destination. A human blind to the future, I could do no more than hope to know. We never even really understand what we have already lived through." (13)<br /><br />--&gt; This description of Batara Kala reminds me of the theory of the Big Bang. Particularly, how after the Big Bang, all the things in the universe move further and further apart, until one day they will stop and shrink back to become one, then explode again. A rebirth. Of course, I am oversimplifying. How did those ancient people get this intuition, that was also theorized by scientists? <br /><br />The description of Batara Kala further sounds like the Big Bang theory, in the next page:<br /><br />"Whether light or shadow, nothing can escape being pushed along by Batara Kala (...) All are pushed on towards that horizon, while it recedes eternally beyond our reach, pushed on towards annihilation. And it is that annihilation that in turn brings rebirth."<br /><br />"And the further Batara Kala pulled us apart, the more I came to feel that truly, I loved her (...) And love, like every other object and situation, has its shadow. And love's shadow is called pain. There is nothing without its shadow except light itself." (14)<br /><br />"In the past peoples could live at peace in the middle of deserts and forests. Now they cannot. Science and modern learning will pursue everyone everywhere. Human beings, both as individuals and social beings, can no longer feel secure. Mankind is forever being pursued because modern science and learning constantly provide the inspiration and desire to control Nature and man together. There is no power that can bring to a halt his passion to control, except greater science and learning, in the hands of more virtuous people." (90) by Khouw Ah Soe<br /><br />--&gt; How true is this quote! How relevant it still remains today, during the times when people are scared of cybersecurity and other technologies. It is also very rare nowadays that someone knows how to survive in the wild. How so very dependent we are on our technologies... and yet how weak they are in the face of disasters, such as COVID-19. How dangerous knowledge is in the hands of corrupt people, such as powerful business owners who exploit the countries (and the workers of those countries) where they extract resources from.<br /><br />"You can ask forgiveness of God at any time at all, if you sin against Him. Sins against your fellow man are different again; it is much more difficult to get him to forgive you. God is all-compassionate; man is uncompassionate." (95) by Robert Mellema<br /><br />"Writing is obviously not just a means towards self-satisfaction. Writing must be a way of giving substance to your life, as Jean said." (189)<br /><br />"Darsam and others like him only realize now: All this time life has depended on those right-hand fingers. Life comes from those fingers. All of a sudden those fingers cannot be used. He only realizes it after his capital is damaged, the living capital of his fingers. Others pretend they work with their brains. For years and years, they study, they learn to think that they might live properly and as they wish to live. But a person's brain can be damaged too; remember Tuan Mellema." (224)<br />by Mama<br /><br />"To run away is to admit you are a criminal. All your education and experience will be in vain." (226)<br /><br />"Willing or unwilling, you will become a part of the cane-crusher machine--like the sieve, or a cog, or a steam kettle." (272)<br /><br />--&gt; These were words by the radical Dutchman that Minke met on the ship. It's a statement that rings true for all the benefits that colonizers brought to the Netherland Indies, such as education. Even though one studies highly such as to become a doctor, it is all to benefit the colonizers, who realize over time that in order to have a more efficient business they need at least some intelligent people. Such as doctors for the plantation and factory workers who get ill, or Native administrators who understand Dutch. I wonder if these words can be applied to today though. Are we all going to college, graduating, working in the industry, to eventually become a part of the metaphorical "cane-crusher machine"? If so, what is that machine exactly? Is it capitalism?<br /><br />"Those without capital are no more than coolies, no more than that, no matter if they are more brilliant than all of the Roman and Greek gods together." (272)<br /><br />--&gt; This is also true in today's world. Notice how those without any sort of capital except their labor, such as the poor, can often get stuck in their poverty traps. It is very hard to get out without capital, such as in the form of education or health.<br /><br />"If one's feelings of humanity are offended (...) everyone with feeling will also be offended, except for people who are mad and those with truly criminal mentalities, even though they may be university graduates." (339)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17005736748816184961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3512077285" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3512077285" />
    <a title="Andrada" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4864790-andrada"><img alt="Andrada" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494861282p2/4864790.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3512077285?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrada" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrada" href="/user/show/4864790-andrada">Andrada</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4864790-andrada?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3512077285" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4600045837190661834">It’s funny in a way that Minke gets accused of making speeches in his writing in this second instalment of the Buru Quartet as it feels like the book itself makes way too many of them. Was Pramoedya Ananta Toer taking a jab at his own writing with it? Maybe?<br/><br/>While in This Earth of Mankind, there was a good balance between plot and political discourse, I feel like Child of All Nations all together forgets at times that it is a fiction book. There are almost chapter-long monologues where characters</span>
  <span id="freeText4600045837190661834" style="display:none">It’s funny in a way that Minke gets accused of making speeches in his writing in this second instalment of the Buru Quartet as it feels like the book itself makes way too many of them. Was Pramoedya Ananta Toer taking a jab at his own writing with it? Maybe?<br /><br />While in This Earth of Mankind, there was a good balance between plot and political discourse, I feel like Child of All Nations all together forgets at times that it is a fiction book. There are almost chapter-long monologues where characters like Kommer and Ter Haar expound on colonial policies and issues. And while I understand they were needed to help open Minke’s eyes to the greater political and economic landscape of the Dutch East Indies, they feel more like standalone lectures than a part of a greater narrative. <br /><br />Because of all this, the plot didn’t advance much. I expected the tetralogy to perhaps deal with all of Minke’s life, from his adolescence onwards, but Child of all Nations barely moves the action along 2-3 years in which, well, nothing much happens except a slow ideological wake-up call. <br />The parts I enjoyed the most were ultimately the ones that told stories, although they both addressed harsh colonial truths: the stories of Surati and Trunodongso. I liked how in Surati’s story you have a parallel to Sanikem’s from the first book, similar but at the same time with vastly different outcomes.<br /><br />During the first book, I was very displeased with the character of Annelies and I was hoping she would have a chance to evolve later on, but that obviously didn’t happen.<br /><br />I’m not sure what to expect from the next two books. More lecturing? Will Minke finally move on and get to Betawi? I hope so! </span>
  <a data-text-id="4600045837190661834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16118205623499437911">Fantastic, as good as the first one in the Buru Tetralogy.<br/>Amazing characters, each person represents a part of the Javanese society and typical problems they were facing- bringing life to the beginning of 20th century in Indonesia on Java. The writer is a smart Javanese student getting Duch education and marrying a beauty from a rich family- Dutch father and Indonesian mother, Nyai the smart, resilient Javanese lady who was sold as a little girl by her father to the Mellema, rich Dutch sugar fac</span>
  <span id="freeText16118205623499437911" style="display:none">Fantastic, as good as the first one in the Buru Tetralogy.<br />Amazing characters, each person represents a part of the Javanese society and typical problems they were facing- bringing life to the beginning of 20th century in Indonesia on Java. The writer is a smart Javanese student getting Duch education and marrying a beauty from a rich family- Dutch father and Indonesian mother, Nyai the smart, resilient Javanese lady who was sold as a little girl by her father to the Mellema, rich Dutch sugar factory manager, she is self taught and tough, but kind....French artist; a mixed blood journalist; local foreman; local landowner that gets his land stolen by Dutch factory manager....a saucy javanese woman working for Nyai that gets pregnant....<br /><br />Pramoedya is an amazing historian, political analyst and critique of his nation under the Dutch colonial rule. He also covers the political turmoil of the other countries in the neighborhood like Philippines, China... <br /><br />This book is a classic and should be made a mandatory read in all schools.<br /><br />Did you know he was in prison during the time he created the 4 books, and at first he memorized the content and told it to his inmates as he wasn't allowed to write. Only towards the end of his sentence he was given writing tools when he wrote the books....<br /><br />Out of this world, detailed, emotionally engaging, intellectual, wonderful, not to be missed for all those that want to learn more about Indonesia!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16118205623499437911" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1564822475&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1564822475" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12731972665330949157">I return to Surabaya, to a more melancholic Minke undergoing a much-needed teething stage as he learns that all that he had received at the prominent Dutch school H. B. S. has become, to quote from Nyai Ontosoroh, "children's games." He struggles to accept that his role as an educated Native is to speak and, ultimately, represent the poor in the Netherlands Indies: the sugar coolies, the peasants, and the farmers long victims of land-grabbing. Coming from a once colonized country, I found myself</span>
  <span id="freeText12731972665330949157" style="display:none">I return to Surabaya, to a more melancholic Minke undergoing a much-needed teething stage as he learns that all that he had received at the prominent Dutch school H. B. S. has become, to quote from Nyai Ontosoroh, "children's games." He struggles to accept that his role as an educated Native is to speak and, ultimately, represent the poor in the Netherlands Indies: the sugar coolies, the peasants, and the farmers long victims of land-grabbing. Coming from a once colonized country, I found myself being called out by the book several instances, that as a writer it is my duty to write for and about my own people. <br /><br />The blaze in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301304.This_Earth_of_Mankind__The_Buru_Quartet___1_" title="This Earth of Mankind (The Buru Quartet, #1) by Pramoedya Ananta Toer" rel="nofollow noopener">This Earth of Mankind</a> has now reduced itself to a low but important glow. The Indies and the nations surrounding it are undergoing incredible change in terms of science, education, and balances of power. Even the Philippine Revolution of 1898 was mentioned! Perhaps it is this milestone in the quartet that Toer, a prisoner of his own country, wanted to emphasize. It's time for the colonized to wake up and realize that the masters they once knew can potentially lose their hold on power. It's up to the very individuals that stand on the skin of their Mother Country to change, rise up, and fight both singularly and collectively. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12731972665330949157" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23822929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4295744940973355970">This is the second book of Pram's Buru Quartet (Bumi Manusia, Anak Semua Bangsa, Jejak langkah, dan Rumah Kaca). The first part of the book did disappoint me in a way when the wife of the main character, Annelies, died after she got separated from her husband by the colonial government. Then, i knew that later on more and more suffers would come to Minke's life (the main character). I think Minke here somehow represents Pram himself who faced almost same situation, suffering because of his strug</span>
  <span id="freeText4295744940973355970" style="display:none">This is the second book of Pram's Buru Quartet (Bumi Manusia, Anak Semua Bangsa, Jejak langkah, dan Rumah Kaca). The first part of the book did disappoint me in a way when the wife of the main character, Annelies, died after she got separated from her husband by the colonial government. Then, i knew that later on more and more suffers would come to Minke's life (the main character). I think Minke here somehow represents Pram himself who faced almost same situation, suffering because of his struggle for his own nation and that suffer didnt seem to meet an end. Yet, Pram showed how Minke did not give up and kept on struggling (as I assumed this was how he also made his own commitment). In this book, we could see how Minke had his own struggle inside about how he should stand against the colonial government, how to stand against any kinds of suppression and injustice.A must read book absolutely!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4295744940973355970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_94893322" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94893322" />
    <a title="Anthony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/154132-anthony"><img alt="Anthony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508824183p2/154132.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94893322?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony" href="/user/show/154132-anthony">Anthony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94893322" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13517033450669505581">The 2nd volume in Pramoedya's Buru Quartet, written while serving in Buru Island Prison Camp as a Suharto regime political prisoner, follows the protagonists Nyai and Minke as Minke's wife Annelies is sent off to the Netherlands as chattel and Nyai's business comes under threat by her dead husband's relative who claims inheritance rights. Minke, a Native, though educated at the prestigious Dutch HBS secondary school, gradually shakes off his idolization of the West and realizes, far later than N</span>
  <span id="freeText13517033450669505581" style="display:none">The 2nd volume in Pramoedya's Buru Quartet, written while serving in Buru Island Prison Camp as a Suharto regime political prisoner, follows the protagonists Nyai and Minke as Minke's wife Annelies is sent off to the Netherlands as chattel and Nyai's business comes under threat by her dead husband's relative who claims inheritance rights. Minke, a Native, though educated at the prestigious Dutch HBS secondary school, gradually shakes off his idolization of the West and realizes, far later than Nyai does, that the Dutch occupation did not intend to civilize the Indies, but to brutalize it and rob it of sugar wealth and autonomy. As such, this volume centers on Minke's transformation, from the death of his wife in transit on an unnecessary journey to Amsterdam, and the forceful but loving prodding of his friends and mother-in-law to use his writing skills to stir the Natives to throw off the yoke of colonial oppression. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13517033450669505581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13823216070758739314">An absolutely amazing book. <br/><br/>I didn't read the first book of the series, since other readers assured that it could be read as an individual book and as it seemed to be the more popular of the group. Perhaps because of this, but also because of the writing style, I was happily lost for a good portion of the book. The author strung me along with tiny details about previous events and deeds that were not expanded upon. This style was so engaging that I couldn't help but be drawn forward. <br/><br/>Besides the</span>
  <span id="freeText13823216070758739314" style="display:none">An absolutely amazing book. <br /><br />I didn't read the first book of the series, since other readers assured that it could be read as an individual book and as it seemed to be the more popular of the group. Perhaps because of this, but also because of the writing style, I was happily lost for a good portion of the book. The author strung me along with tiny details about previous events and deeds that were not expanded upon. This style was so engaging that I couldn't help but be drawn forward. <br /><br />Besides the alluring style, the content was fantastic! The author effectively captured the evolution of his opinion regarding the societal status quo in Dutch-ruled Indonesia. His awakening of consciousness to the institutionalized marginalization of racial class structures was perfection. From confusion, to slight openings, quickly becoming overwhelmed, and lastly a burgeoning sense of his potential role or place in potential future change. <br /><br />I would highly recommend this book to other readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13823216070758739314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2575882396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16983766504374059685">A good read. Since this is the second book in a series of four, the 'cheatsheat' in the introduction made it easy to know who the very interesting characters were in this continuing story. "Written" orally from stories the author told his fellow prison inmates, this book in particular is of a Javanese young man with a Dutch education coming to grips with the injustice of the colonial empire that runs his country. <br/>Now I want to read the other three books in this quartet!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UPISLpalEt/Wfoqkhb6ONUp5lD3zWkWsSd9UPtVix1vCNTZOXKsFyQ1L0aDvrJX4zFMDDjwDvXxs9g8314beTQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Max is an inspirational figure, but he is also a flawed idealist whose vow to protect the Javanese from cruelty ends in his own downfall. In Max Havelaar, Multatuli (the pseudonym for Eduard Douwes Dekker) vividly recreated his own experiences in Java and tellingly depicts the hypocrisy of those who gained from the corrupt coffee trade. Sending shockwaves through the Dutch nation when it was published in 1860, this damning exposé of the terrible conditions in the colonies led to welfare reforms in Java and continues to inspire the fairtrade movement today.\n\nRoy Edwards\'s vibrant translation conveys the satirical and innovative style of Multatuli\'s autobiographical polemic. It is at o­nce an exposé of the oppression of plantation workers in South Sumatra, a lyrical quest to understand the place of religion and spirituality in contemporary lives, a playful exploration of female sexuality and a story about love in all its guises, while touching o­n all of Indonesia’s taboos: extramarital sex, political repression and the relationship between Christians and Muslims.Saman has taken the Indonesian literary world by storm and sold over 100,000 copies in the Indonesian language, and is now available for the first time in English.ABOUT THE AUTHORAyu Utami was born in Bogor, grew up in Jakarta and obtained her bachelor degree in Literature Studies from University of Indonesia. She worked as a journalist for Matra, Forum Keadilan, and D&amp;R. Not long after the New Order regime closed Tempo, Editor, and Detik, she participated in the founding of Indonesia’s Alliance of Independent Journalists to protest the closure of those three weeklys. Currently she is working for the cultural journal Kalam, and at Teater Utan Kayu. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4159683673042851245">Pramoedya Ananta Toer was an Indonesian author of novels, short stories, essays, polemics, and histories of his homeland and its people. A well-regarded writer in the West, Pramoedya's outspoken and often politically charged writings faced censorship in his native land during the pre-reformation era. For opposing the policies of both founding president Sukarno, as well as those of its successor, t</span>
  <span id="freeText4159683673042851245" style="display:none">Pramoedya Ananta Toer was an Indonesian author of novels, short stories, essays, polemics, and histories of his homeland and its people. A well-regarded writer in the West, Pramoedya's outspoken and often politically charged writings faced censorship in his native land during the pre-reformation era. For opposing the policies of both founding president Sukarno, as well as those of its successor, the New Order regime of Suharto, he faced extrajudicial punishment. During the many years in which he suffered imprisonment and house arrest, he became a cause célèbre for advocates of freedom of expression and human rights.<br /><br />Bibliography:<br />* Kranji-Bekasi Jatuh (1947)<br />* Perburuan (The Fugitive) (1950)<br />* Keluarga Gerilya (1950)<br />* Bukan Pasarmalam (1951)<br />* Cerita dari Blora (1952)<br />* Gulat di Jakarta (1953)<br />* Korupsi (Corruption) (1954)<br />* Midah - Si Manis Bergigi Emas (1954)<br />* Cerita Calon Arang (The King, the Witch, and the Priest) (1957)<br />* Hoakiau di Indonesia (1960)<br />* Panggil Aku Kartini Saja I &amp; II (1962)<br />* The Buru Quartet<br /> o Bumi Manusia (This Earth of Mankind) (1980)<br /> o Anak Semua Bangsa (Child of All Nations) (1980)<br /> o Jejak Langkah (Footsteps) (1985)<br /> o Rumah Kaca (House of Glass) (1988)<br />* Gadis Pantai (The Girl from the Coast) (1982)<br />* Nyanyi Sunyi Seorang Bisu (A Mute's Soliloquy) (1995)<br />* Arus Balik (1995)<br />* Arok Dedes (1999)<br />* Mangir (1999)<br />* Larasati (2000)<br /></span>
